<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Influenza</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>

<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Infectious disease, "the flu"</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Influenza_(disambiguation)" title="Influenza (disambiguation)">Influenza (disambiguation)</a>, <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Flu_(disambiguation)" title="Flu (disambiguation)">Flu (disambiguation)</a>, and <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Grippe_(disambiguation)" title="Grippe (disambiguation)">Grippe (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Flus" redirects here. For the diagnostic class of thyroid nodules, see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FLUS" title="FLUS">FLUS</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Flue" title="Flue">Flue</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>

class="infobox-label" scope="row">Deaths</th><td class="infobox-data">&gt;290,000–650,000 deaths per year<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allen_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-allen-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lampejo_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-lampejo-4">[4]</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Influenza</b>, commonly known as "<b>the flu</b>", is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infectious_disease" title="Infectious disease">infectious disease</a> caused by <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Influenza_viruses" title="Influenza viruses">influenza viruses</a></i>. Symptoms range from mild to severe and often include fever, runny nose, sore throat, muscle pain, headache, coughing, and fatigue. These symptoms begin from one to four days after exposure to the virus (typically two days) and last for about 2–8 days. Diarrhea and vomiting can occur, particularly in children. Influenza may progress to <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a>, which can be caused by the virus or by a subsequent bacterial infection. Other complications of infection include <a href="/wiki/Acute_respiratory_distress_syndrome" title="Acute respiratory distress syndrome">acute respiratory distress syndrome</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meningitis" title="Meningitis">meningitis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Encephalitis" title="Encephalitis">encephalitis</a>, and worsening of pre-existing health problems such as <a href="/wiki/Asthma" title="Asthma">asthma</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_disease" title="Cardiovascular disease">cardiovascular disease</a>.
</p><p>There are four types of influenza virus, termed influenza viruses A, B, C, and D. Aquatic birds are the primary source of <i><a href="/wiki/Influenza_A_virus" title="Influenza A virus">Influenza A virus</a></i> (IAV), which is also widespread in various mammals, including humans and pigs. <i><a href="/wiki/Influenza_B_virus" title="Influenza B virus">Influenza B virus</a></i> (IBV) and <i><a href="/wiki/Influenza_C_virus" title="Influenza C virus">Influenza C virus</a></i> (ICV) primarily infect humans, and <i><a href="/wiki/Influenza_D_virus" title="Influenza D virus">Influenza D virus</a></i> (IDV) is found in cattle and pigs. IAV and IBV circulate in humans and cause seasonal epidemics, and ICV causes a mild infection, primarily in children. IDV can infect humans but is not known to cause illness. In humans, influenza viruses are primarily transmitted through respiratory droplets produced from coughing and sneezing. Transmission through aerosols and intermediate objects and surfaces contaminated by the virus  also occur.
</p><p>Frequent hand washing and covering one's mouth and nose when coughing and sneezing reduce transmission. Annual vaccination can help to provide protection against influenza. Influenza viruses, particularly IAV, evolve quickly, so flu vaccines are updated regularly to match which influenza strains are in circulation. Vaccines currently in use provide protection against IAV subtypes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H1N1" title="H1N1">H1N1</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H3N2" title="H3N2">H3N2</a> and one or two IBV subtypes. Influenza infection is diagnosed with laboratory methods such as  <a href="/wiki/Antibody" title="Antibody">antibody</a> or <a href="/wiki/Antigen" title="Antigen">antigen</a> tests and a polymerase chain reaction (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PCR" title="PCR">PCR</a>) to identify viral nucleic acid. The disease can be treated with supportive measures and, in severe cases, with antiviral drugs such as <a href="/wiki/Oseltamivir" title="Oseltamivir">oseltamivir</a>. In healthy individuals, influenza is typically self-limiting and rarely fatal, but it can be deadly in high-risk groups.
</p><p>In a typical year, 5–15% of the population contracts influenza. There are 3–5 million severe cases annually, with up to 650,000 respiratory-related deaths globally each year. Deaths most commonly occur in high-risk groups, including young children, the elderly, and people with chronic health conditions. In temperate regions of the world, the number of influenza cases peaks during winter, whereas in the tropics influenza can occur year-round. Since the late 1800s, large outbreaks of novel influenza strains that spread globally, called pandemics, have occurred every 10–50 years. Five flu pandemics have occurred since 1900: the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_flu" title="Spanish flu">Spanish flu</a> in 1918–1920, which was the most severe flu pandemic, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asian_flu" title="Asian flu">Asian flu</a> in 1957, the <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong_flu" title="Hong Kong flu">Hong Kong flu</a> in 1968, the <a href="/wiki/1977_Russian_flu" title="1977 Russian flu">Russian flu</a> in 1977, and the <a href="/wiki/2009_swine_flu_pandemic" title="2009 swine flu pandemic">swine flu pandemic</a> in 2009.
</p>

</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Signs_and_symptoms">Signs and symptoms</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Symptoms_of_influenza.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1050" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="263" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Symptoms_of_influenza.svg/300px-Symptoms_of_influenza.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Symptoms_of_influenza.svg/450px-Symptoms_of_influenza.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Symptoms_of_influenza.svg/600px-Symptoms_of_influenza.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Symptoms_of_influenza.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Symptoms of influenza,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> with fever and cough the most common symptoms<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15728170_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15728170-7">[7]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The time between exposure to the virus and development of symptoms, called the <a href="/wiki/Incubation_period" title="Incubation period">incubation period</a>, is 1–4 days, most commonly 1–2 days. Many infections, however, are asymptomatic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dharmapalan_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-dharmapalan-8">[8]</a></sup> The onset of symptoms is sudden, and initial symptoms are predominately non-specific, including fever, chills, headaches, <a href="/wiki/Myalgia" title="Myalgia">muscle pain or aching</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malaise" title="Malaise">a feeling of discomfort</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anorexia" title="Anorexia">loss of appetite</a>, lack of energy/fatigue, and confusion. These symptoms are usually accompanied by respiratory symptoms such as a dry cough, sore or dry throat, hoarse voice, and a stuffy or runny nose. Coughing is the most common symptom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> Gastrointestinal symptoms may also occur, including nausea, vomiting, diarrhea,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ghebrehewet_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-ghebrehewet-9">[9]</a></sup> and gastroenteritis,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sederdahl_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-sederdahl-10">[10]</a></sup> especially in children. The standard influenza symptoms typically last for 2–8 days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peteranderl_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-peteranderl-11">[11]</a></sup>  A 2021 study suggests influenza can cause long lasting symptoms in a similar way to <a href="/wiki/Long_COVID" title="Long COVID">long COVID</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>Symptomatic infections are usually mild and limited to the upper respiratory tract, but progression to pneumonia is relatively common. Pneumonia may be caused by the primary viral infection or by a secondary bacterial infection. Primary pneumonia is characterized by rapid progression of fever, cough, labored breathing, and low oxygen levels that cause bluish skin. It is especially common among those who have an underlying cardiovascular disease such as rheumatic heart disease. Secondary pneumonia typically has a period of improvement in symptoms for 1–3 weeks<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kalil_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-kalil-13">[13]</a></sup> followed by recurrent fever, sputum production, and fluid buildup in the lungs,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> but can also occur just a few days after influenza symptoms appear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kalil_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-kalil-13">[13]</a></sup> About a third of primary pneumonia cases are followed by secondary pneumonia, which is most frequently caused by the bacteria <i><a href="/wiki/Streptococcus_pneumoniae" title="Streptococcus pneumoniae">Streptococcus pneumoniae</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Staphylococcus_aureus" title="Staphylococcus aureus">Staphylococcus aureus</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dharmapalan_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-dharmapalan-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Virology">Virology</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Types_of_virus">Types of virus</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Influenza_nomenclature.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="380" data-file-width="590" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Influenza_nomenclature.svg/310px-Influenza_nomenclature.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Influenza_nomenclature.svg/465px-Influenza_nomenclature.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Influenza_nomenclature.svg/620px-Influenza_nomenclature.svg.png 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Influenza_nomenclature.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Influenza virus <a href="/wiki/International_Committee_on_Taxonomy_of_Viruses" title="International Committee on Taxonomy of Viruses">nomenclature</a> (for a <a href="/wiki/Fujian_flu" title="Fujian flu">Fujian flu</a> virus)</div></div></div>
<p>Influenza viruses comprise four species. Each of the four species is the sole member of its own genus, and the four influenza genera comprise four of the seven genera in the family <i><a href="/wiki/Orthomyxoviridae" title="Orthomyxoviridae">Orthomyxoviridae</a></i>. They are:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ictv_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-ictv-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Influenza_A_virus" title="Influenza A virus">Influenza A virus</a></i> (IAV), genus <i>Alphainfluenzavirus</i></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Influenza_B_virus" title="Influenza B virus">Influenza B virus</a></i> (IBV), genus <i>Betainfluenzavirus</i></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Influenza_C_virus" title="Influenza C virus">Influenza C virus</a></i> (ICV), genus <i>Gammainfluenzavirus</i></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Influenza_D_virus" title="Influenza D virus">Influenza D virus</a></i> (IDV), genus <i>Deltainfluenzavirus</i></li></ul>
<p>IAV is responsible for most cases of severe illness as well as seasonal epidemics and occasional pandemics. It infects people of all ages but tends to disproportionately cause severe illness in the elderly, the very young, and those who have chronic health issues. Birds are the primary reservoir of IAV, especially aquatic birds such as ducks, geese, shorebirds, and gulls,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-li_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-li-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-joseph_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-joseph-16">[16]</a></sup> but the virus also circulates among mammals, including pigs, horses, and marine mammals. IAV is classified into subtypes based on the viral proteins <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haemagglutinin" title="Haemagglutinin">haemagglutinin</a> (H) and <a href="/wiki/Neuraminidase" title="Neuraminidase">neuraminidase</a> (N).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asha_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-asha-17">[17]</a></sup> As of 2019, 18 H subtypes and 11 N subtypes have been identified. Most potential combinations have been reported in birds, but H17-18 and N10-11 have only been found in bats. Only H subtypes H1-3 and N subtypes N1-2 are known to have circulated in humans,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asha_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-asha-17">[17]</a></sup> the current IAV subtypes in circulation being H1N1 and H3N2.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sautto_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-sautto-2">[2]</a></sup> IAVs can be classified more specifically to also include natural host species, geographical origin, year of isolation, and strain number, such as H1N1/A/duck/Alberta/35/76.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ghebrehewet_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-ghebrehewet-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>IBV mainly infects humans but has been identified in seals, horses, dogs, and pigs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asha_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-asha-17">[17]</a></sup> IBV does not have subtypes like IAV but has two antigenically distinct lineages, termed the B/Victoria/2/1987-like and B/Yamagata/16/1988-like lineages,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-6"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> or simply (B/)Victoria(-like) and (B/)Yamagata(-like).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asha_17-3"><a href="#cite_note-asha-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sautto_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-sautto-2">[2]</a></sup> Both lineages are in circulation in humans,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-7"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> disproportionately affecting children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ghebrehewet_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-ghebrehewet-9">[9]</a></sup> IBVs contribute to seasonal epidemics alongside IAVs but have never been associated with a pandemic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asha_17-4"><a href="#cite_note-asha-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>ICV, like IBV, is primarily found in humans, though it also has been detected in pigs, feral dogs, <a href="/wiki/Dromedary" title="Dromedary">dromedary</a> camels, cattle, and dogs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sederdahl_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-sederdahl-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asha_17-5"><a href="#cite_note-asha-17">[17]</a></sup> ICV infection primarily affects children and is usually asymptomatic<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-8"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ghebrehewet_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-ghebrehewet-9">[9]</a></sup> or has mild cold-like symptoms, though more severe symptoms such as gastroenteritis and pneumonia can occur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sederdahl_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-sederdahl-10">[10]</a></sup> Unlike IAV and IBV, ICV has not been a major focus of research pertaining to antiviral drugs, vaccines, and other measures against influenza.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asha_17-6"><a href="#cite_note-asha-17">[17]</a></sup> ICV is subclassified into six genetic/antigenic lineages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sederdahl_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-sederdahl-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-su_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-su-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>IDV has been isolated from pigs and cattle, the latter being the natural reservoir. Infection has also been observed in humans, horses, dromedary camels, and small ruminants such as goats and sheep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asha_17-7"><a href="#cite_note-asha-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-su_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-su-18">[18]</a></sup> IDV is distantly related to ICV. While cattle workers have occasionally tested positive to prior IDV infection, it is not known to cause disease in humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-9"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ghebrehewet_9-4"><a href="#cite_note-ghebrehewet-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sederdahl_10-4"><a href="#cite_note-sederdahl-10">[10]</a></sup> ICV and IDV experience a slower rate of antigenic evolution than IAV and IBV. Because of this antigenic stability, relatively few novel lineages emerge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-su_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-su-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genome_and_structure">Genome and structure</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:3D_Influenza_virus.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="264" data-file-width="356" decoding="async" height="230" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/3D_Influenza_virus.png/310px-3D_Influenza_virus.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3b/3D_Influenza_virus.png 1.5x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:3D_Influenza_virus.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Structure of the influenza <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/virion" title="wikt:virion">virion</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Hemagglutinin" title="Hemagglutinin">hemagglutinin</a> (HA) and <a href="/wiki/Neuraminidase" title="Neuraminidase">neuraminidase</a> (NA) proteins are shown on the surface of the particle. The viral RNAs that make up the <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a> are shown as red coils inside the particle and bound to <a href="/wiki/Influenza_virus_nucleoprotein" title="Influenza virus nucleoprotein">ribonucleoproteins</a> (RNP).</div></div></div>
<p>Influenza viruses have a <a href="/wiki/Sense_(molecular_biology)" title="Sense (molecular biology)">negative-sense</a>, single-stranded RNA genome that is segmented. The negative sense of the genome means it can be used as a template to synthesize <a href="/wiki/Messenger_RNA" title="Messenger RNA">messenger RNA</a> (mRNA).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dharmapalan_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-dharmapalan-8">[8]</a></sup> IAV and IBV have eight genome segments that encode 10 major proteins. ICV and IDV have seven genome segments that encode nine major proteins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sederdahl_10-5"><a href="#cite_note-sederdahl-10">[10]</a></sup> Three segments encode three subunits of an <a href="/wiki/RNA-dependent_RNA_polymerase" title="RNA-dependent RNA polymerase">RNA-dependent RNA polymerase</a> (RdRp) complex: PB1, a transcriptase, PB2, which recognizes 5' caps, and PA (P3 for ICV and IDV), an endonuclease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mccauley_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-mccauley-19">[19]</a></sup> The matrix protein (M1) and membrane protein (M2) share a segment, as do the non-structural protein (NS1) and the nuclear export protein (NEP).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-10"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> For IAV and IBV, hemagglutinin (HA) and neuraminidase (NA) are encoded on one segment each, whereas ICV and IDV encode a hemagglutinin-esterase fusion (HEF) protein on one segment that merges the functions of HA and NA. The final genome segment encodes the viral nucleoprotein (NP).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mccauley_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-mccauley-19">[19]</a></sup> Influenza viruses also encode various accessory proteins, such as PB1-F2 and PA-X, that are <a href="/wiki/Gene_expression" title="Gene expression">expressed</a> through alternative <a href="/wiki/Open_reading_frame" title="Open reading frame">open reading frames</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-11"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shim_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-shim-20">[20]</a></sup> and which are important in host defense suppression, virulence, and pathogenicity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hao_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-hao-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>The virus particle, called a virion, is pleomorphic and varies between being filamentous, bacilliform, or spherical in shape. Clinical isolates tend to be pleomorphic, whereas strains adapted to laboratory growth typically produce spherical virions. Filamentous virions are about 250 nanometers (nm) by 80 nm, bacilliform 120–250 by 95 nm, and spherical 120 nm in diameter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dadonaite_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-dadonaite-22">[22]</a></sup> The virion consists of each segment of the genome bound to nucleoproteins in separate ribonucleoprotein (RNP) complexes for each segment, all of which are surrounded by a lipid bilayer membrane called the <a href="/wiki/Viral_envelope" title="Viral envelope">viral envelope</a>. There is a copy of the RdRp, all subunits included, bound to each RNP. The envelope is reinforced structurally by matrix proteins on the interior that enclose the RNPs,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allen_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-allen-3">[3]</a></sup> and the envelope contains HA and NA (or HEF<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-su_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-su-18">[18]</a></sup>) proteins extending outward from the exterior surface of the envelope. HA and HEF<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-su_18-4"><a href="#cite_note-su-18">[18]</a></sup> proteins have a distinct "head" and "stalk" structure. M2 proteins form proton ion channels through the viral envelope that are required for viral entry and exit. IBVs contain a surface protein named NB that is anchored in the envelope, but its function is unknown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-12"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_cycle">Life cycle</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Virus_Replication_large.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="852" data-file-width="925" decoding="async" height="286" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Virus_Replication_large.svg/310px-Virus_Replication_large.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Virus_Replication_large.svg/465px-Virus_Replication_large.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Virus_Replication_large.svg/620px-Virus_Replication_large.svg.png 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Virus_Replication_large.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Host cell invasion and replication by the influenza virus</div></div></div>
<p>The viral life cycle begins by binding to a target cell. Binding is mediated by the viral HA proteins on the surface of the evelope, which bind to cells that contain sialic acid receptors on the surface of the cell membrane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-13"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-li_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-li-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allen_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-allen-3">[3]</a></sup> For N1 subtypes with the "G147R" mutation and N2 subtypes, the NA protein can initiate entry. Prior to binding, NA proteins promote access to target cells by degrading mucous, which helps to remove extracellular decoy receptors that would impede access to target cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allen_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-allen-3">[3]</a></sup> After binding, the virus is internalized into the cell by an <a href="/wiki/Endosome" title="Endosome">endosome</a> that contains the virion inside it. The endosome is acidified by cellular vATPase<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shim_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-shim-20">[20]</a></sup> to have lower pH, which triggers a conformational change in HA that allows fusion of the viral envelope with the endosomal membrane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hao_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-hao-21">[21]</a></sup> At the same time, hydrogen ions diffuse into the virion through M2 ion channels, disrupting internal protein-protein interactions to release RNPs into the host cell's <a href="/wiki/Cytosol" title="Cytosol">cytosol</a>. The M1 protein shell surrounding RNPs is degraded, fully uncoating RNPs in the cytosol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shim_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-shim-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allen_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-allen-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>RNPs are then imported into the nucleus with the help of viral localization signals. There, the viral RNA polymerase <a href="/wiki/Transcription_(biology)" title="Transcription (biology)">transcribes</a> mRNA using the genomic negative-sense strand as a template. The polymerase <a href="/wiki/Cap_snatching" title="Cap snatching">snatches 5' caps</a> for viral mRNA from cellular RNA to prime mRNA synthesis and the 3'-end of mRNA is polyadenylated at the end of transcription.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mccauley_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-mccauley-19">[19]</a></sup> Once viral mRNA is transcribed, it is exported out of the nucleus and <a href="/wiki/Translation_(biology)" title="Translation (biology)">translated</a> by host ribosomes in a cap-dependent manner to synthesize viral proteins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shim_20-3"><a href="#cite_note-shim-20">[20]</a></sup> RdRp also synthesizes complementary positive-sense strands of the viral genome in a complementary RNP complex which are then used as templates by viral polymerases to synthesize copies of the negative-sense genome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-14"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allen_3-5"><a href="#cite_note-allen-3">[3]</a></sup> During these processes, RdRps of avian influenza viruses (AIVs) function optimally at a higher temperature than mammalian influenza viruses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peteranderl_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-peteranderl-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>Newly synthesized viral polymerase subunits and NP proteins are imported to the nucleus to further increase the rate of viral replication and form RNPs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mccauley_19-3"><a href="#cite_note-mccauley-19">[19]</a></sup> HA, NA, and M2 proteins are trafficked with the aid of M1 and NEP proteins<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hao_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-hao-21">[21]</a></sup> to the cell membrane through the <a href="/wiki/Golgi_apparatus" title="Golgi apparatus">Golgi apparatus</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mccauley_19-4"><a href="#cite_note-mccauley-19">[19]</a></sup> and inserted into the cell's membrane. Viral non-structural proteins including NS1, PB1-F2, and PA-X regulate host cellular processes to disable antiviral responses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-15"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hao_21-3"><a href="#cite_note-hao-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allen_3-6"><a href="#cite_note-allen-3">[3]</a></sup> PB1-F2 aso interacts with PB1 to keep polymerases in the nucleus longer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-joseph_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-joseph-16">[16]</a></sup> M1 and NEP proteins localize to the nucleus during the later stages of infection, bind to viral RNPs and mediate their export to the cytoplasm where they migrate to the cell membrane with the aid of recycled endosomes and are bundled into the segments of the genome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-16"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allen_3-7"><a href="#cite_note-allen-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Progenic viruses leave the cell by budding from the cell membrane, which is initiated by the accumulation of M1 proteins at the cytoplasmic side of the membrane. The viral genome is incorporated inside a viral envelope derived from portions of the cell membrane that have HA, NA, and M2 proteins. At the end of budding, HA proteins remain attached to cellular sialic acid until they are cleaved by the sialidase activity of NA proteins. The virion is then released from the cell. The sialidase activity of NA also cleaves any sialic acid residues from the viral surface, which helps prevent newly assembled viruses from aggregating near the cell surface and improving infectivity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-17"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allen_3-8"><a href="#cite_note-allen-3">[3]</a></sup> Similar to other aspects of influenza replication, optimal NA activity is temperature- and pH-dependent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peteranderl_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-peteranderl-11">[11]</a></sup> Ultimately, presence of large quantities of viral RNA in the cell triggers apoptosis, i.e. programmed cell death, which is initiated by cellular factors to restrict viral replication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shim_20-4"><a href="#cite_note-shim-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antigenic_drift_and_shift">Antigenic drift and shift</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Influenza_geneticshift.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="520" data-file-width="426" decoding="async" height="269" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Influenza_geneticshift.svg/220px-Influenza_geneticshift.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Influenza_geneticshift.svg/330px-Influenza_geneticshift.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Influenza_geneticshift.svg/440px-Influenza_geneticshift.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Influenza_geneticshift.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Antigenic shift, or reassortment, can result in novel and highly pathogenic strains of human influenza.</div></div></div>
<p>Two key processes that influenza viruses evolve through are <a href="/wiki/Antigenic_drift" title="Antigenic drift">antigenic drift</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antigenic_shift" title="Antigenic shift">antigenic shift</a>. Antigenic drift is when an influenza virus's antigens change due to the gradual accumulation of mutations in the antigen's (HA or NA) gene.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-li_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-li-15">[15]</a></sup> This can occur in response to <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_pressure" title="Evolutionary pressure">evolutionary pressure</a> exerted by the host immune response. Antigenic drift is especially common for the HA protein, in which just a few amino acid changes in the head region can constitute antigenic drift.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sautto_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-sautto-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-su_18-5"><a href="#cite_note-su-18">[18]</a></sup> The result is the production of novel strains that can evade pre-existing antibody-mediated immunity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-18"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ghebrehewet_9-5"><a href="#cite_note-ghebrehewet-9">[9]</a></sup> Antigenic drift occurs in all influenza species but is slower in B than A and slowest in C and D.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-su_18-6"><a href="#cite_note-su-18">[18]</a></sup> Antigenic drift is a major cause of seasonal influenza,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hastings_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-hastings-23">[23]</a></sup> and requires that flu vaccines be updated annually. HA is the main component of inactivated vaccines, so surveillance monitors antigenic drift of this antigen among circulating strains. Antigenic evolution of influenza viruses of humans appears to be faster than influenza viruses in swine and equines. In wild birds, within-subtype antigenic variation appears to be limited but has been observed in poultry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-19"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ghebrehewet_9-6"><a href="#cite_note-ghebrehewet-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>Antigenic shift is a sudden, drastic change in an influenza virus's antigen, usually HA. During antigenic shift, antigenically different strains that infect the same cell can <a href="/wiki/Reassortment" title="Reassortment">reassort</a> genome segments with each other, producing hybrid progeny. Since all influenza viruses have segmented genomes, all are capable of reassortment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sederdahl_10-6"><a href="#cite_note-sederdahl-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-su_18-7"><a href="#cite_note-su-18">[18]</a></sup> Antigenic shift, however, only occurs among influenza viruses of the same genus<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mccauley_19-5"><a href="#cite_note-mccauley-19">[19]</a></sup> and most commonly occurs among IAVs. In particular, reassortment is very common in AIVs, creating a large diversity of influenza viruses in birds, but is uncommon in human, equine, and canine lineages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lycett_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-lycett-24">[24]</a></sup> Pigs, bats, and quails have receptors for both mammalian and avian IAVs, so they are potential "mixing vessels" for reassortment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asha_17-8"><a href="#cite_note-asha-17">[17]</a></sup> If an animal strain reassorts with a human strain,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sautto_2-4"><a href="#cite_note-sautto-2">[2]</a></sup> then a novel strain can emerge that is capable of human-to-human transmission. This has caused pandemics, but only a limited number have occurred, so it is difficult to predict when the next will happen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-20"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ghebrehewet_9-7"><a href="#cite_note-ghebrehewet-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanism">Mechanism</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transmission">Transmission</span></h3>
<p>People who are infected can transmit influenza viruses through breathing, talking, coughing, and sneezing, which spread <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_droplet" title="Respiratory droplet">respiratory droplets</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aerosol" title="Aerosol">aerosols</a> that contain virus particles into the air. A person susceptible to infection can then contract influenza by coming into contact with these particles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kalil_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-kalil-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kutter_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-kutter-25">[25]</a></sup> Respiratory droplets are relatively large and travel less than two meters before falling onto nearby surfaces. Aerosols are smaller and remain suspended in the air longer, so they take longer to settle and can travel further than respiratory droplets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kutter_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-kutter-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lampejo_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-lampejo-4">[4]</a></sup> Inhalation of aerosols can lead to infection,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-killingley_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-killingley-26">[26]</a></sup> but most transmission is in the area about two meters around an infected person via respiratory droplets<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dharmapalan_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-dharmapalan-8">[8]</a></sup> that come into contact with mucosa of the upper respiratory tract.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-killingley_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-killingley-26">[26]</a></sup> Transmission through contact with a person, bodily fluids, or intermediate objects (<a href="/wiki/Fomite" title="Fomite">fomites</a>) can also occur, such as through contaminated hands and surfaces<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dharmapalan_8-4"><a href="#cite_note-dharmapalan-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kutter_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-kutter-25">[25]</a></sup> since influenza viruses can survive for hours on non-porous surfaces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lampejo_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-lampejo-4">[4]</a></sup> If one's hands are contaminated, then touching one's face can cause infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weber_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-weber-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>Influenza is usually transmissible from one day before the onset of symptoms to 5–7 days after.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ghebrehewet_9-8"><a href="#cite_note-ghebrehewet-9">[9]</a></sup> In healthy adults, the virus is shed for up to 3–5 days. In children and the immunocompromised, the virus may be transmissible for several weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dharmapalan_8-5"><a href="#cite_note-dharmapalan-8">[8]</a></sup> Children ages 2–17 are considered to be the primary and most efficient spreaders of influenza.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-21"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ghebrehewet_9-9"><a href="#cite_note-ghebrehewet-9">[9]</a></sup> Children who have not had multiple prior exposures to influenza viruses shed the virus at greater quantities and for a longer duration than other children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-22"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> People who are at risk of exposure to influenza include health care workers, social care workers, and those who live with or care for people vulnerable to influenza. In long-term care facilities, the flu can spread rapidly after it is introduced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ghebrehewet_9-10"><a href="#cite_note-ghebrehewet-9">[9]</a></sup> A variety of factors likely encourage influenza transmission, including lower temperature, lower absolute and relative <a href="/wiki/Humidity" title="Humidity">humidity</a>, less <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> radiation from the Sun,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-killingley_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-killingley-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-moriyama_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-moriyama-28">[28]</a></sup> and crowding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kutter_25-3"><a href="#cite_note-kutter-25">[25]</a></sup> Influenza viruses that infect the upper respiratory tract like H1N1 tend to be more mild but more transmissible, whereas those that infect the lower respiratory tract like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H5N1" title="H5N1">H5N1</a> tend to cause more severe illness but are less contagious.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dharmapalan_8-6"><a href="#cite_note-dharmapalan-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pathophysiology">Pathophysiology</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:H1N1_versus_H5N1_pathology.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="552" data-file-width="754" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/H1N1_versus_H5N1_pathology.png/310px-H1N1_versus_H5N1_pathology.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/H1N1_versus_H5N1_pathology.png/465px-H1N1_versus_H5N1_pathology.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/H1N1_versus_H5N1_pathology.png/620px-H1N1_versus_H5N1_pathology.png 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:H1N1_versus_H5N1_pathology.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>How the different sites of infection (shown in red) of H1N1 and H5N1 influences their transmission and lethality</div></div></div>
<p>In humans, influenza viruses first cause infection by infecting epithelial cells in the respiratory tract. Illness during infection is primarily the result of lung <a href="/wiki/Inflammation" title="Inflammation">inflammation</a> and compromise caused by epithelial cell infection and death, combined with inflammation caused by the immune system's response to infection. Non-respiratory organs can become involved, but the mechanisms by which influenza is involved in these cases are unknown. Severe respiratory illness can be caused by multiple, non-exclusive mechanisms, including obstruction of the airways, loss of <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_alveolus" title="Pulmonary alveolus">alveolar</a> structure, loss of lung epithelial integrity due to epithelial cell infection and death, and degradation of the extracellular matrix that maintains lung structure. In particular, alveolar cell infection appears to drive severe symptoms since this results in impaired gas exchange and enables viruses to infect endothelial cells, which produce large quantities of pro-inflammatory <a href="/wiki/Cytokine" title="Cytokine">cytokines</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kalil_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-kalil-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>Pneumonia caused by influenza viruses is characterized by high levels of viral replication in the lower respiratory tract, accompanied by a strong pro-inflammatory response called a <a href="/wiki/Cytokine_storm" title="Cytokine storm">cytokine storm</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-23"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> Infection with H5N1 or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H7N9" title="H7N9">H7N9</a> especially produces high levels of pro-inflammatory cytokines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-li_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-li-15">[15]</a></sup> In bacterial infections, early depletion of <a href="/wiki/Macrophage" title="Macrophage">macrophages</a> during influenza creates a favorable environment in the lungs for bacterial growth since these white blood cells are important in responding to bacterial infection. Host mechanisms to encourage tissue repair may inadvertently allow bacterial infection. Infection also induces production of systemic <a href="/wiki/Glucocorticoid" title="Glucocorticoid">glucocorticoids</a> that can reduce inflammation to preserve tissue integrity but allow increased bacterial growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kalil_13-4"><a href="#cite_note-kalil-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>The pathophysiology of influenza is significantly influenced by which receptors influenza viruses bind to during entry into cells. Mammalian influenza viruses preferentially bind to sialic acids connected to the rest of the oligosaccharide by an α-2,6 link, most commonly found in various respiratory cells,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-24"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-li_15-4"><a href="#cite_note-li-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allen_3-9"><a href="#cite_note-allen-3">[3]</a></sup> such as respiratory and retinal epithelial cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shim_20-5"><a href="#cite_note-shim-20">[20]</a></sup> AIVs prefer sialic acids with an α-2,3 linkage, which are most common in birds in gastrointestinal epithelial cells<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-25"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-li_15-5"><a href="#cite_note-li-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allen_3-10"><a href="#cite_note-allen-3">[3]</a></sup> and in humans in the lower respiratory tract.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shao_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-shao-29">[29]</a></sup> Furthermore, <a href="/wiki/Proteolysis" title="Proteolysis">cleavage</a> of the HA protein into HA<sub>1</sub>, the binding subunit, and HA<sub>2</sub>, the fusion subunit, is performed by different proteases, affecting which cells can be infected. For mammalian influenza viruses and low pathogenic AIVs, cleavage is extracellular, which limits infection to cells that have the appropriate proteases, whereas for highly pathogenic AIVs, cleavage is intracellular and performed by ubiquitous proteases, which allows for infection of a greater variety of cells, thereby contributing to more severe disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-26"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lycett_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-lycett-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-steinhauer_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-steinhauer-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Immunology">Immunology</span></h3>
<p>Cells possess sensors to detect viral RNA, which can then induce <a href="/wiki/Interferon" title="Interferon">interferon</a> production. Interferons mediate expression of antiviral proteins and proteins that recruit immune cells to the infection site, and they also notify nearby uninfected cells of infection. Some infected cells release pro-inflammatory cytokines that recruit immune cells to the site of infection. Immune cells control viral infection by killing infected cells and <a href="/wiki/Phagocytosis" title="Phagocytosis">phagocytizing</a> viral particles and apoptotic cells. An exacerbated immune response, however, can harm the host organism through a cytokine storm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-27"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peteranderl_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-peteranderl-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shim_20-6"><a href="#cite_note-shim-20">[20]</a></sup> To counter the immune response, influenza viruses encode various non-structural proteins, including NS1, NEP, PB1-F2, and PA-X, that are involved in curtailing the host immune response by suppressing interferon production and host gene expression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-28"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hao_21-4"><a href="#cite_note-hao-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/B_cell" title="B cell">B cells</a>, a type of white blood cell, produce antibodies that bind to influenza antigens HA and NA (or HEF<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-su_18-8"><a href="#cite_note-su-18">[18]</a></sup>) and other proteins to a lesser degree. Once bound to these proteins, antibodies block virions from binding to cellular receptors, <a href="/wiki/Neutralizing_antibody" title="Neutralizing antibody">neutralizing</a> the virus. In humans, a sizeable antibody response occurs ~1 week after viral exposure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> This antibody response is typically robust and long-lasting, especially for ICV and IDV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-29"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-su_18-9"><a href="#cite_note-su-18">[18]</a></sup> In other words, people exposed to a certain strain in childhood still possess antibodies to that strain at a reasonable level later in life, which can provide some protection to related strains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-30"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> There is, however, an "<a href="/wiki/Original_antigenic_sin" title="Original antigenic sin">original antigenic sin</a>", in which the first HA subtype a person is exposed to influences the antibody-based immune response to future infections and vaccines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sautto_2-5"><a href="#cite_note-sautto-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prevention">Prevention</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Influenza_prevention" title="Influenza prevention">Influenza prevention</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vaccination">Vaccination</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Influenza_vaccine" title="Influenza vaccine">Influenza vaccine</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Defense.gov_News_Photo_041028-N-9864S-021.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1960" data-file-width="3008" decoding="async" height="202" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Defense.gov_News_Photo_041028-N-9864S-021.jpg/310px-Defense.gov_News_Photo_041028-N-9864S-021.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Defense.gov_News_Photo_041028-N-9864S-021.jpg/465px-Defense.gov_News_Photo_041028-N-9864S-021.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Defense.gov_News_Photo_041028-N-9864S-021.jpg/620px-Defense.gov_News_Photo_041028-N-9864S-021.jpg 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Defense.gov_News_Photo_041028-N-9864S-021.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Giving an influenza vaccination</div></div></div>
<p>Annual vaccination is the primary and most effective way to prevent influenza and influenza-associated complications, especially for high-risk groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dharmapalan_8-7"><a href="#cite_note-dharmapalan-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-31"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chow_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-chow-32">[32]</a></sup> Vaccines against the flu are trivalent or quadrivalent, providing protection against an H1N1 strain, an H3N2 strain, and one or two IBV strains corresponding to the two IBV lineages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dharmapalan_8-8"><a href="#cite_note-dharmapalan-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sautto_2-6"><a href="#cite_note-sautto-2">[2]</a></sup> Two types of vaccines are in use: inactivated vaccines that contain "killed" (i.e. inactivated) viruses and live attenuated influenza vaccines (LAIVs) that contain weakened viruses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-32"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> There are three types of inactivated vaccines: whole virus, split virus, in which the virus is disrupted by a detergent, and subunit, which only contains the viral antigens HA and NA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tregoning_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-tregoning-33">[33]</a></sup> Most flu vaccines are inactivated and administered via intramuscular injection. LAIVs are sprayed into the nasal cavity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-33"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>Vaccination recommendations vary by country. Some recommend vaccination for all people above a certain age, such as 6 months,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chow_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-chow-32">[32]</a></sup> whereas other countries recommendation is limited for high at risk groups, such as pregnant women, young children (excluding newborns), the elderly, people with chronic medical conditions, health care workers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-34"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> people who come into contact with high-risk people, and people who transmit the virus easily.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ghebrehewet_9-11"><a href="#cite_note-ghebrehewet-9">[9]</a></sup> Young infants cannot receive flu vaccines for safety reasons, but they can inherit <a href="/wiki/Passive_immunity" title="Passive immunity">passive immunity</a> from their mother if inactivated vaccines are administered to the mother during pregnancy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-principi_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-principi-34">[34]</a></sup> Influenza vaccination also helps to reduce the probability of reassortment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peteranderl_11-4"><a href="#cite_note-peteranderl-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>In general, influenza vaccines are only effective if there is an antigenic match between vaccine strains and circulating strains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dharmapalan_8-9"><a href="#cite_note-dharmapalan-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sautto_2-7"><a href="#cite_note-sautto-2">[2]</a></sup> Additionally, most commercially available flu vaccines are manufactured by propagation of influenza viruses in embryonated chicken eggs, taking 6–8 months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sautto_2-8"><a href="#cite_note-sautto-2">[2]</a></sup> Flu seasons are different in the northern and southern hemisphere, so the WHO meets twice a year, one for each hemisphere, to discuss which strains should be included in flu vaccines based on observation from HA inhibition assays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dharmapalan_8-10"><a href="#cite_note-dharmapalan-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allen_3-11"><a href="#cite_note-allen-3">[3]</a></sup> Other manufacturing methods include an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Madin-Darby_Canine_Kidney_cells" title="Madin-Darby Canine Kidney cells">MDCK cell</a> culture-based inactivated vaccine and a recombinant subunit vaccine manufactured from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baculovirus" title="Baculovirus">baculovirus</a> overexpression in insect cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sautto_2-9"><a href="#cite_note-sautto-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barr_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-barr-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antiviral_chemoprophylaxis">Antiviral chemoprophylaxis</span></h3>
<p>Influenza can be prevented or reduced in severity by post-exposure prophylaxis with the antiviral drugs <a href="/wiki/Oseltamivir" title="Oseltamivir">oseltamivir</a>, which can be taken orally by those at least three months old, and <a href="/wiki/Zanamivir" title="Zanamivir">zanamivir</a>, which can be inhaled by those above seven years of age. <a href="/wiki/Chemoprophylaxis" title="Chemoprophylaxis">Chemoprophylaxis</a> is most useful for individuals at high-risk of developing complications and those who cannot receive the flu vaccine due to contraindications or lack of effectiveness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dharmapalan_8-11"><a href="#cite_note-dharmapalan-8">[8]</a></sup> Post-exposure chemoprophylaxis is only recommended if oseltamivir is taken within 48 hours of contact with a confirmed or suspected influenza case and zanamivir within 36 hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dharmapalan_8-12"><a href="#cite_note-dharmapalan-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ghebrehewet_9-12"><a href="#cite_note-ghebrehewet-9">[9]</a></sup> It is recommended that it be offered to people who have yet to receive a vaccine for the current flu season, who have been vaccinated less than two week since contact, if there is a significant mismatch between vaccine and circulating strains, or during an outbreak in a closed setting regardless of vaccination history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ghebrehewet_9-13"><a href="#cite_note-ghebrehewet-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Infection_control">Infection control</span></h3>
<p>Hand hygiene is important in reducing the spread of influenza. This includes frequent hand washing with soap and water, using <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_(chemistry)" title="Alcohol (chemistry)">alcohol</a>-based <a href="/wiki/Hand_sanitizer" title="Hand sanitizer">hand sanitizers</a>, and not touching one's eyes, nose, and mouth with one's hands. Covering one's nose and mouth when coughing or sneezing is important.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jefferson_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-jefferson-36">[36]</a></sup> Other methods to limit influenza transmission include staying home when sick,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-35"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> avoiding contact with others until one day after symptoms end,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ghebrehewet_9-14"><a href="#cite_note-ghebrehewet-9">[9]</a></sup> and disinfecting surfaces likely to be contaminated by the virus, such as doorknobs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-36"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> Health education through media and posters is often used to remind people of the aforementioned etiquette and hygiene.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dharmapalan_8-13"><a href="#cite_note-dharmapalan-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is uncertainty about the use of masks since research thus far has not shown a significant reduction in seasonal influenza with mask usage. Likewise, the effectiveness of screening at points of entry into countries is not well researched.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jefferson_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-jefferson-36">[36]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Social_distancing" title="Social distancing">Social distancing</a> measures such as school closures, avoiding contact with infected people via isolation or quarantine, and limiting mass gatherings may reduce transmission,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-37"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jefferson_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-jefferson-36">[36]</a></sup> but these measures are often expensive, unpopular, and difficult to implement. Consequently, the commonly recommended methods of infection control are respiratory etiquette, hand hygiene, and mask wearing, which are inexpensive and easy to perform. Pharmaceutical measures are effective but may not be available in the early stages of an outbreak.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-saunders_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-saunders-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>In health care settings, infected individuals may be cohorted or assigned to individual rooms. Protective clothing such as masks, gloves, and gowns is recommended when coming into contact with infected individuals if there is a risk of exposure to infected bodily fluids. Keeping patients in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Negative_pressure_room" title="Negative pressure room">negative pressure rooms</a> and avoiding aerosol-producing activities may help,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dharmapalan_8-14"><a href="#cite_note-dharmapalan-8">[8]</a></sup> but special <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air_handling" title="Air handling">air handling</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ventilation_(architecture)" title="Ventilation (architecture)">ventilation</a> systems are not considered necessary to prevent the spread of influenza in the air.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lampejo_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-lampejo-4">[4]</a></sup> In residential homes, new admissions may need to be closed until the spread of influenza is controlled. When discharging patients to care homes, it is important to take care if there is a known influenza outbreak.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ghebrehewet_9-15"><a href="#cite_note-ghebrehewet-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since influenza viruses circulate in animals such as birds and pigs, prevention of transmission from these animals is important. <a href="/wiki/Water_treatment" title="Water treatment">Water treatment</a>, indoor raising of animals, quarantining sick animals, vaccination, and <a href="/wiki/Biosecurity" title="Biosecurity">biosecurity</a> are the primary measures used. Placing poultry houses and piggeries on high ground away from high-density farms, backyard farms, live poultry markets, and bodies of water helps to minimize contact with wild birds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-38"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> Closure of live poultry markets appears to the most effective measure<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-li_15-6"><a href="#cite_note-li-15">[15]</a></sup> and has shown to be effective at controlling the spread of H5N1, H7N9, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H9N2" title="H9N2">H9N2</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-joseph_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-joseph-16">[16]</a></sup> Other biosecurity measures include cleaning and disinfecting facilities and vehicles, banning visits to poultry farms, not bringing birds intended for slaughter back to farms,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-avian_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-avian-38">[38]</a></sup> changing clothes, disinfecting foot baths, and treating food and water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-39"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>If live poultry markets are not closed, then "clean days" when unsold poultry is removed and facilities are disinfected and "no carry-over" policies to eliminate infectious material before new poultry arrive can be used to reduce the spread of influenza viruses. If a novel influenza viruses has breached the aforementioned biosecurity measures, then rapid detection to stamp it out via quarantining, decontamination, and culling may be necessary to prevent the virus from becoming endemic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-40"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> Vaccines exist for avian H5, H7, and H9 subtypes that are used in some countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-li_15-7"><a href="#cite_note-li-15">[15]</a></sup> In China, for example, vaccination of domestic birds against H7N9 successfully limited its spread, indicating that vaccination may be an effective strategy<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lycett_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-lycett-24">[24]</a></sup> if used in combination with other measures to limit transmission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-41"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> In pigs and horses, management of influenza is dependent on vaccination with biosecurity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-42"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diagnosis">Diagnosis</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flu.png.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="483" data-file-width="632" decoding="async" height="237" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Flu.png.jpg/310px-Flu.png.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Flu.png.jpg/465px-Flu.png.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Flu.png.jpg/620px-Flu.png.jpg 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flu.png.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>X-ray of 29-year-old person with H1N1</div></div></div>
<p>Diagnosis based on symptoms is fairly accurate in otherwise healthy people during seasonal epidemics and should be suspected in cases of pneumonia, acute respiratory distress syndrome (ARDS), <a href="/wiki/Sepsis" title="Sepsis">sepsis</a>, or if encephalitis, <a href="/wiki/Myocarditis" title="Myocarditis">myocarditis</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Rhabdomyolysis" title="Rhabdomyolysis">breaking down of muscle tissue</a> occur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kalil_13-5"><a href="#cite_note-kalil-13">[13]</a></sup> Because influenza is similar to other viral respiratory tract illnesses, laboratory diagnosis is necessary for confirmation. Common ways of collecting samples for testing include nasal and throat swabs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-43"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> Samples may be taken from the lower respiratory tract if infection has cleared the upper but not lower respiratory tract. Influenza testing is recommended for anyone hospitalized with symptoms resembling influenza during flu season or who is connected to an influenza case. For severe cases, earlier diagnosis improves patient outcome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chow_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-chow-32">[32]</a></sup> Diagnostic methods that can identify influenza include <a href="/wiki/Viral_culture" title="Viral culture">viral cultures</a>, antibody- and antigen-detecting tests, and nucleic acid-based tests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vemula_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-vemula-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>Viruses can be grown in a culture of mammalian cells or <a href="/wiki/Embryonated" title="Embryonated">embryonated</a> eggs for 3–10 days to monitor cytopathic effect. Final confirmation can then be done via antibody staining, hemadsorption using <a href="/wiki/Red_blood_cell" title="Red blood cell">red blood cells</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Immunofluorescence" title="Immunofluorescence">immunofluorescence</a> microscopy. Shell vial cultures, which can identify infection via immunostaining before a cytopathic effect appears, are more sensitive than traditional cultures with results in 1–3 days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-44"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chow_32-3"><a href="#cite_note-chow-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vemula_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-vemula-39">[39]</a></sup> Cultures can be used to characterize novel viruses, observe sensitivity to antiviral drugs, and monitor antigenic drift, but they are relatively slow and require specialized skills and equipment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-45"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>Serological assays can be used to detect an antibody response to influenza after natural infection or vaccination. Common serological assays include hemagglutination inhibition assays that detect HA-specific antibodies, virus neutralization assays that check whether antibodies have neutralized the virus, and enzyme-linked immunoabsorbant assays. These methods tend to be relatively inexpensive and fast but are less reliable than nucleic-acid based tests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-46"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vemula_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-vemula-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>Direct fluorescent or immunofluorescent antibody (DFA/IFA) tests involve staining respiratory epithelial cells in samples with fluorescently-labeled influenza-specific antibodies, followed by examination under a fluorescent microscope. They can differentiate between IAV and IBV but can't subtype IAV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vemula_39-3"><a href="#cite_note-vemula-39">[39]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rapid_influenza_diagnostic_test" title="Rapid influenza diagnostic test">Rapid influenza diagnostic tests</a> (RIDTs) are a simple way of obtaining assay results, are low cost, and produce results quickly, at less than 30 minutes, so they are commonly used, but they can't distinguish between IAV and IBV or between IAV subtypes and are not as sensitive as nucleic-acid based tests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-47"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vemula_39-4"><a href="#cite_note-vemula-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nucleic acid-based tests (NATs) amplify and detect viral nucleic acid. Most of these tests take a few hours,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vemula_39-5"><a href="#cite_note-vemula-39">[39]</a></sup> but rapid molecular assays are as fast as RIDTs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chow_32-4"><a href="#cite_note-chow-32">[32]</a></sup> Among NATs, <a href="/wiki/Reverse_transcription_polymerase_chain_reaction" title="Reverse transcription polymerase chain reaction">reverse transcription polymerase chain reaction</a> (RT-PCR) is the most traditional and considered the gold standard for diagnosing influenza<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vemula_39-6"><a href="#cite_note-vemula-39">[39]</a></sup> because it is fast and can subtype IAV, but it is relatively expensive and more prone to false-positives than cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-48"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> Other NATs that have been used include <a href="/wiki/Loop-mediated_isothermal_amplification" title="Loop-mediated isothermal amplification">loop-mediated isothermal amplification</a>-based assays, simple amplification-based assays, and nucleic acid sequence-based amplification. Nucleic acid sequencing methods can identify infection by obtaining the nucleic acid sequence of viral samples to identify the virus and antiviral drug resistance. The traditional method is <a href="/wiki/Sanger_sequencing" title="Sanger sequencing">Sanger sequencing</a>, but it has been largely replaced by <a href="/wiki/Massive_parallel_sequencing" title="Massive parallel sequencing">next-generation methods</a> that have greater sequencing speed and throughput.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vemula_39-7"><a href="#cite_note-vemula-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Treatment">Treatment</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Influenza_treatment" title="Influenza treatment">Influenza treatment</a></div>
<p>Treatment of influenza in cases of mild or moderate illness is supportive and includes anti-fever medications such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acetaminophen" title="Acetaminophen">acetaminophen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ibuprofen" title="Ibuprofen">ibuprofen</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MLP2010_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-MLP2010-40">[40]</a></sup> adequate fluid intake to avoid dehydration, and resting at home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ghebrehewet_9-16"><a href="#cite_note-ghebrehewet-9">[9]</a></sup> Cough drops and throat sprays may be beneficial for sore throat. It is recommended to avoid alcohol and tobacco use while sick with the flu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MLP2010_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-MLP2010-40">[40]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Aspirin" title="Aspirin">Aspirin</a> is not recommended to treat influenza in children due to an elevated risk of developing Reye syndrome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-banday_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-banday-41">[41]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Corticosteroid" title="Corticosteroid">Corticosteroids</a> likewise are not recommended except when treating <a href="/wiki/Septic_shock" title="Septic shock">septic shock</a> or an underlying medical condition, such as <a href="/wiki/Chronic_obstructive_pulmonary_disease" title="Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease">chronic obstructive pulmonary disease</a> or asthma exacerbation, since they are associated with increased mortality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chow_32-5"><a href="#cite_note-chow-32">[32]</a></sup> If a secondary bacterial infection occurs, then treatment with antibiotics may be necessary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ghebrehewet_9-17"><a href="#cite_note-ghebrehewet-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antivirals">Antivirals</span></h3>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center; float:right; font-size:85%; margin-left:15px">
<caption>Antiviral drugs<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peteranderl_11-5"><a href="#cite_note-peteranderl-11">[11]</a></sup>
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th>Drug</th>
<th>Route of administration</th>
<th>Approved age of use
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td>Oseltamivir</td>
<td>Oral</td>
<td>At least two weeks old
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Zanamivir</td>
<td>Inhalation</td>
<td>At least five years old
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Peramivir</td>
<td>Intravenous injection</td>
<td>At least 18 years old
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Laninamivir</td>
<td>Inhalation<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-49"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup></td>
<td>40 milligrams (mg) dose for people at least 10 years old,<br/> 20 mg for those under 10<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lanina_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-lanina-42">[42]</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Baloxavir marboxil</td>
<td>Oral<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lampejo_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-lampejo-4">[4]</a></sup></td>
<td>At least 12 years old<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chow_32-6"><a href="#cite_note-chow-32">[32]</a></sup>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Antiviral drugs are primarily used to treat severely ill patients, especially those with compromised immune systems. Antivirals are most effective when started in the first 48 hours after symptoms appear. Later administration may still be beneficial for those who have underlying immune defects, those with more severe symptoms, or those who have a higher risk of developing complications if these individuals are still shedding the virus. Antiviral treatment is also recommended if a person is hospitalized with suspected influenza instead of waiting for test results to return and if symptoms are worsening.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-50"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chow_32-7"><a href="#cite_note-chow-32">[32]</a></sup> Most antiviral drugs against influenza fall into two categories: neuraminidase (NA) inhibitors and M2 inhibitors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peteranderl_11-6"><a href="#cite_note-peteranderl-11">[11]</a></sup> Baloxavir marboxil is a notable exception, which targets the endonuclease activity of the viral RNA polymerase and can be used as an alternative to NA and M2 inhibitors for IAV and IBV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dharmapalan_8-15"><a href="#cite_note-dharmapalan-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-li_15-8"><a href="#cite_note-li-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lampejo_4-5"><a href="#cite_note-lampejo-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>NA inhibitors target the enzymatic activity of NA receptors, mimicking the binding of sialic acid in the active site of NA on IAV and IBV virions<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-51"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> so that viral release from infected cells and the rate of viral replication are impaired.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ghebrehewet_9-18"><a href="#cite_note-ghebrehewet-9">[9]</a></sup> NA inhibitors include oseltamivir, which is consumed orally in a prodrug form and converted to its active form in the liver, and zanamivir, which is a powder that is inhaled nasally. Oseltamivir and zanamivir are effective for prophylaxis and post-exposure prophylaxis, and research overall indicates that NA inhibitors are effective at reducing rates of complications, hospitalization, and mortality<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-52"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> and the duration of illness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peteranderl_11-7"><a href="#cite_note-peteranderl-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chow_32-8"><a href="#cite_note-chow-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lampejo_4-6"><a href="#cite_note-lampejo-4">[4]</a></sup> Additionally, the earlier NA inhibitors are provided, the better the outcome,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lampejo_4-7"><a href="#cite_note-lampejo-4">[4]</a></sup> though late administration can still be beneficial in severe cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-53"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chow_32-9"><a href="#cite_note-chow-32">[32]</a></sup> Other NA inhibitors include laninamivir<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-54"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> and peramivir, the latter of which can be used as an alternative to oseltamivir for people who cannot tolerate or absorb it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chow_32-10"><a href="#cite_note-chow-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Adamantane" title="Adamantane">adamantanes</a> <a href="/wiki/Amantadine" title="Amantadine">amantadine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rimantadine" title="Rimantadine">rimantadine</a> are orally administered drugs that block the influenza virus's M2 ion channel,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-55"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> preventing viral uncoating.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lampejo_4-8"><a href="#cite_note-lampejo-4">[4]</a></sup> These drugs are only functional against IAV<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chow_32-11"><a href="#cite_note-chow-32">[32]</a></sup> but are no longer recommended for use because of widespread resistance to them among IAVs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lampejo_4-9"><a href="#cite_note-lampejo-4">[4]</a></sup> Adamantane resistance first emerged in H3N2 in 2003, becoming worldwide by 2008. Oseltamivir resistance is no longer widespread because the 2009 pandemic H1N1 strain (H1N1 pdm09), which is resistant to adamantanes, seemingly replaced resistant strains in circulation. Since the 2009 pandemic, oseltamivir resistance has mainly been observed in patients undergoing therapy,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-56"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> especially the immunocompromised and young children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lampejo_4-10"><a href="#cite_note-lampejo-4">[4]</a></sup> Oseltamivir resistance is usually reported in H1N1, but has been reported in H3N2 and IBVs less commonly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-57"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> Because of this, oseltamivir is recommended as the first drug of choice for immunocompetent people, whereas for the immunocompromised, oseltamivir is recommended against H3N2 and IBV and zanamivir against H1N1 pdm09. Zanamivir resistance is observed less frequently, and resistance to peramivir and baloxavir marboxil is possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lampejo_4-11"><a href="#cite_note-lampejo-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prognosis">Prognosis</span></h2>
