<p>In healthy individuals, influenza infection is usually self-limiting and rarely fatal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dharmapalan_8-16"><a href="#cite_note-dharmapalan-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ghebrehewet_9-19"><a href="#cite_note-ghebrehewet-9">[9]</a></sup> Symptoms usually last for 2–8 days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peteranderl_11-8"><a href="#cite_note-peteranderl-11">[11]</a></sup> Influenza can cause people to miss work or school, and it is associated with decreased job performance and, in older adults, reduced independence. Fatigue and malaise may last for several weeks after recovery, and healthy adults may experience pulmonary abnormalities that can take several weeks to resolve. Complications and mortality primarily occur in high-risk populations and those who are hospitalized. Severe disease and mortality are usually attributable to pneumonia from the primary viral infection or a secondary bacterial infection,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-58"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ghebrehewet_9-20"><a href="#cite_note-ghebrehewet-9">[9]</a></sup> which can progress to ARDS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peteranderl_11-9"><a href="#cite_note-peteranderl-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other respiratory complications that may occur include <a href="/wiki/Sinusitis" title="Sinusitis">sinusitis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bronchitis" title="Bronchitis">bronchitis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bronchiolitis" title="Bronchiolitis">bronchiolitis</a>, excess fluid buildup in the lungs, and exacerbation of chronic bronchitis and asthma. <a href="/wiki/Otitis_media" title="Otitis media">Middle ear infection</a> and <a href="/wiki/Croup" title="Croup">croup</a> may occur, most commonly in children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dharmapalan_8-17"><a href="#cite_note-dharmapalan-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-59"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> Secondary <i>S. aureus</i> infection has been observed, primarily in children, to cause <a href="/wiki/Toxic_shock_syndrome" title="Toxic shock syndrome">toxic shock syndrome</a> after influenza, with hypotension, fever, and reddening and peeling of the skin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-60"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> Complications affecting the cardiovascular system are rare and include pericarditis, fulminant myocarditis with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arrythmia" title="Arrythmia">fast, slow, or irregular heartbeat</a>, and exacerbation of pre-existing cardiovascular disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dharmapalan_8-18"><a href="#cite_note-dharmapalan-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ghebrehewet_9-21"><a href="#cite_note-ghebrehewet-9">[9]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Myositis" title="Myositis">Inflammation or swelling of muscles</a> accompanied by muscle tissue breaking down occurs rarely, usually in children, which presents as extreme tenderness and muscle pain in the legs and a reluctance to walk for 2–3 days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-61"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ghebrehewet_9-22"><a href="#cite_note-ghebrehewet-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kalil_13-6"><a href="#cite_note-kalil-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>Influenza can affect pregnancy, including causing smaller neonatal size, increased risk of premature birth, and an increased risk of child death shortly before or after birth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ghebrehewet_9-23"><a href="#cite_note-ghebrehewet-9">[9]</a></sup> Neurological complications have been associated with influenza on rare occasions, including aseptic meningitis, encephalitis, disseminated encephalomyelitis, transverse myelitis, and <a href="/wiki/Guillain%E2%80%93Barr%C3%A9_syndrome" title="Guillain–Barré syndrome">Guillain–Barré syndrome</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kalil_13-7"><a href="#cite_note-kalil-13">[13]</a></sup> Additionally, febrile seizures and Reye syndrome can occur, most commonly in children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-62"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ghebrehewet_9-24"><a href="#cite_note-ghebrehewet-9">[9]</a></sup> Influenza-associated encephalopathy can occur directly from central nervous system infection from <a href="/wiki/Viremia" title="Viremia">the presence of the virus in blood</a> and presents as suddent onset of fever with convulsions, followed by rapid progression to coma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dharmapalan_8-19"><a href="#cite_note-dharmapalan-8">[8]</a></sup> An atypical form of encephalitis called encephalitis lethargica, characterized by headache, drowsiness, and coma, may rarely occur sometime after infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-63"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> In survivors of influenza-associated encephalopathy, neurological defects may occur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dharmapalan_8-20"><a href="#cite_note-dharmapalan-8">[8]</a></sup> Primarily in children, in severe cases the immune system may rarely <a href="/wiki/Hemophagocytic_lymphohistiocytosis" title="Hemophagocytic lymphohistiocytosis">dramatically overproduce white blood cells</a> that release cytokines, causing severe inflammation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dharmapalan_8-21"><a href="#cite_note-dharmapalan-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>People who are at least 65 years of age,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ghebrehewet_9-25"><a href="#cite_note-ghebrehewet-9">[9]</a></sup> due to a weakened immune system from aging or a chronic illness, are a high-risk group for developing complications, as are children less than one year of age and children who have not been previously exposed to influenza viruses multiple times. Pregnant women are at an elevated risk, which increases by trimester<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-64"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> and lasts up to two weeks after childbirth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ghebrehewet_9-26"><a href="#cite_note-ghebrehewet-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chow_32-12"><a href="#cite_note-chow-32">[32]</a></sup> Obesity, in particular a <a href="/wiki/Body_mass_index" title="Body mass index">body mass index</a> greater than 35–40, is associated with greater amounts of viral replication, increased severity of secondary bacterial infection, and reduced vaccination efficacy. People who have underlying health conditions are also considered at-risk, including those who have congenital or chronic heart problems or lung (e.g. asthma), kidney, liver, blood, neurological, or metabolic (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Diabetes" title="Diabetes">diabetes</a>) disorders,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dharmapalan_8-22"><a href="#cite_note-dharmapalan-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-65"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ghebrehewet_9-27"><a href="#cite_note-ghebrehewet-9">[9]</a></sup> as are people who are immunocompromised from chemotherapy, <a href="/wiki/Asplenia" title="Asplenia">asplenia</a>, prolonged steroid treatment, splenic dysfunction, or <a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">HIV</a> infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ghebrehewet_9-28"><a href="#cite_note-ghebrehewet-9">[9]</a></sup> Current or past tobacco use also places a person at risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chow_32-13"><a href="#cite_note-chow-32">[32]</a></sup> The role of genetics in influenza is not well researched,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-66"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> but it may be a factor in influenza mortality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peteranderl_11-10"><a href="#cite_note-peteranderl-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Epidemiology">Epidemiology</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Flu_season" title="Flu season">Flu season</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flu_pandemics" title="Flu pandemics">Flu pandemics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:InfluenzaCaseMortality.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="248" data-file-width="300" decoding="async" height="231" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/InfluenzaCaseMortality.svg/280px-InfluenzaCaseMortality.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/InfluenzaCaseMortality.svg/420px-InfluenzaCaseMortality.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/InfluenzaCaseMortality.svg/560px-InfluenzaCaseMortality.svg.png 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:InfluenzaCaseMortality.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Influenza mortality in symptomatic cases in the US for the 2018/2019 season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Influenza is typically characterized by seasonal epidemics and sporadic pandemics. Most of the burden of influenza is a result of flu seasons caused by IAV and IBV. Among IAV subtypes, H1N1 and H3N2 currently circulate in humans and are responsible for seasonal influenza. Cases disproportionately occur in children, but most severe causes are among the elderly, the very young,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-67"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> and the immunocompromised.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lampejo_4-12"><a href="#cite_note-lampejo-4">[4]</a></sup> In a typical year, influenza viruses infect 5–15% of the global population,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allen_3-12"><a href="#cite_note-allen-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vemula_39-8"><a href="#cite_note-vemula-39">[39]</a></sup> causing 3–5 million cases of severe illness annually<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-68"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sautto_2-10"><a href="#cite_note-sautto-2">[2]</a></sup> and accounting for 290,000–650,000 deaths each year due to respiratory illness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allen_3-13"><a href="#cite_note-allen-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lampejo_4-13"><a href="#cite_note-lampejo-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> 5–10% of adults and 20–30% of children contract influenza each year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asha_17-9"><a href="#cite_note-asha-17">[17]</a></sup> The reported number of influenza cases is usually much lower than the actual number of cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-69"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-principi_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-principi-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>During seasonal epidemics, it is estimated that about 80% of otherwise healthy people who have a cough or sore throat have the flu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-70"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> Approximately 30–40% of people hospitalized for influenza develop pneumonia, and about 5% of all severe pneumonia cases in hospitals are due to influenza, which is also the most common cause of ARDS in adults. In children, influenza is one of the two most common causes of ARDS, the other being the <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_syncytial_virus" title="Respiratory syncytial virus">respiratory syncytial virus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kalil_13-8"><a href="#cite_note-kalil-13">[13]</a></sup> About 3–5% of children each year develop otitis media due to influenza.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dharmapalan_8-23"><a href="#cite_note-dharmapalan-8">[8]</a></sup> Adults who develop organ failure from influenza and children who have PIM scores and acute renal failure have higher rates of mortality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kalil_13-9"><a href="#cite_note-kalil-13">[13]</a></sup> During seasonal influenza, mortality is concentrated in the very young and the elderly, whereas during flu pandemics, young adults are often affected at a high rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peteranderl_11-11"><a href="#cite_note-peteranderl-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Influenza_Seasonal_Risk_Areas.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="443" data-file-width="863" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Influenza_Seasonal_Risk_Areas.svg/310px-Influenza_Seasonal_Risk_Areas.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Influenza_Seasonal_Risk_Areas.svg/465px-Influenza_Seasonal_Risk_Areas.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Influenza_Seasonal_Risk_Areas.svg/620px-Influenza_Seasonal_Risk_Areas.svg.png 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Influenza_Seasonal_Risk_Areas.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Seasonal risk areas for influenza: November–April (blue), April–November (red), and year-round (yellow)</div></div></div>
<p>In temperate regions, the number of influenza cases varies from season to season. Lower <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_D" title="Vitamin D">vitamin D</a> levels, presumably due to less sunlight,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-moriyama_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-moriyama-28">[28]</a></sup> lower humidity, lower temperature, and minor changes in virus proteins caused by antigenic drift contribute to annual epidemics that peak during the winter season. In the northern hemisphere, this is from October to May (more narrowly December to April<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peteranderl_11-12"><a href="#cite_note-peteranderl-11">[11]</a></sup>), and in the southern hemisphere, this is from May to October (more narrowly June to September<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peteranderl_11-13"><a href="#cite_note-peteranderl-11">[11]</a></sup>). There are therefore two distinct influenza seasons every year in temperate regions, one in the northern hemisphere and one in the southern hemisphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-71"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ghebrehewet_9-29"><a href="#cite_note-ghebrehewet-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sautto_2-11"><a href="#cite_note-sautto-2">[2]</a></sup>  In tropical and subtropical regions, seasonality is more complex and appears to be affected by various climatic factors such as minimum temperature, hours of sunshine, maximum rainfall, and high humidity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-72"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-potter_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-potter-45">[45]</a></sup> Influenza may therefore occur year-round in these regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peteranderl_11-14"><a href="#cite_note-peteranderl-11">[11]</a></sup> Influenza epidemics in modern times have the tendency to start in the eastern or southern hemisphere,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-potter_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-potter-45">[45]</a></sup> with Asia being a key reservoir of influenza viruses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peteranderl_11-15"><a href="#cite_note-peteranderl-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>IAV and IBV co-circulate, so the two have the same patterns of transmission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-73"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> The seasonality of ICV, however, is poorly understood. ICV infection is most common in children under the age of 2, and by adulthood most people have been exposed to it. ICV-associated hospitalization most commonly occurs in children under the age of 3 and is frequently accompanied by co-infection with another virus or a bacterium, which may increase the severity of disease. When considering all hospitalizations for respiratory illness among young children, ICV appears to account for only a small percentage of such cases. Large outbreaks of ICV infection can occur, so incidence varies significantly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sederdahl_10-7"><a href="#cite_note-sederdahl-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>Outbreaks of influenza caused by novel influenza viruses are common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mccauley_19-6"><a href="#cite_note-mccauley-19">[19]</a></sup> Depending on the level of pre-existing immunity in the population, novel influenza viruses can spread rapidly and cause pandemics with millions of deaths. These pandemics, in contrast to seasonal influenza, are caused by antigenic shifts involving animal influenza viruses. To date, all known flu pandemics have been caused by IAVs, and they follow the same pattern of spreading from an origin point to the rest of the world over the course of multiple waves in a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-74"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ghebrehewet_9-30"><a href="#cite_note-ghebrehewet-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chow_32-14"><a href="#cite_note-chow-32">[32]</a></sup> Pandemic strains tend to be associated with higher rates of pneumonia in otherwise healthy individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kalil_13-10"><a href="#cite_note-kalil-13">[13]</a></sup> Generally after each influenza pandemic, the pandemic strain continues to circulate as the cause of seasonal influenza, replacing prior strains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-75"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> From 1700 to 1889, influenza pandemics occurred about once every 50–60 years. Since then, pandemics have occurred about once every 10–50 years, so they may be getting more frequent over time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-potter_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-potter-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Influenza_subtypes.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="250" data-file-width="631" decoding="async" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Influenza_subtypes.svg/350px-Influenza_subtypes.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Influenza_subtypes.svg/525px-Influenza_subtypes.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Influenza_subtypes.svg/700px-Influenza_subtypes.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Influenza_subtypes.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The main types of influenza viruses in humans. Solid squares show the appearance of a new strain, causing recurring influenza pandemics. Broken lines indicate uncertain strain identifications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Palese_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Palese-46">[46]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>It is impossible to know when an influenza virus first infected humans or when the first influenza pandemic occurred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morens2_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-morens2-47">[47]</a></sup> Possibly the first influenza epidemic occurred around 6,000 BC in China,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mordini_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-mordini-48">[48]</a></sup> and possible descriptions of influenza exist in Greek writings from the 5th century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-potter_45-3"><a href="#cite_note-potter-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-martin_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-martin-49">[49]</a></sup> In both 1173–1174 AD and 1387 AD, epidemics occurred across Europe that were named "influenza". Whether these epidemics and others were caused by influenza is unclear since there was no consistent naming pattern for epidemic respiratory diseases at that time, and "influenza" didn't become completely attached to respiratory disease until centuries later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morens1_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-morens1-50">[50]</a></sup> Influenza may have been brought to the Americas as early as 1493, when an epidemic disease resembling influenza killed most of the population of the <a href="/wiki/Antilles" title="Antilles">Antilles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guerra1988_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-guerra1988-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guerra1993_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-guerra1993-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first convincing record of an influenza pandemic was <a href="/wiki/1510_influenza_pandemic" title="1510 influenza pandemic">chronicled in 1510</a>; it began in East Asia before spreading to North Africa and then Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morens2_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-morens2-47">[47]</a></sup> Following the pandemic, seasonal influenza occurred, with subsequent pandemics in 1557 and 1580.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morens1_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-morens1-50">[50]</a></sup> The flu pandemic in 1557 was potentially the first time influenza was connected to miscarriage and death of pregnant women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-knobler_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-knobler-53">[53]</a></sup> The 1580 flu pandemic originated in Asia during summer, spread to Africa, then Europe, and finally America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-potter_45-4"><a href="#cite_note-potter-45">[45]</a></sup> By the end of the 16th century, influenza was likely beginning to become understood as a specific, recognizable disease with epidemic and endemic forms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morens1_50-2"><a href="#cite_note-morens1-50">[50]</a></sup> In 1648, it was discovered that horses also experience influenza.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morens2_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-morens2-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>Influenza data after 1700 is more informative, so it is easier to identify flu pandemics after this point, each of which incrementally increased understanding of influenza.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taubenberger_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-taubenberger-54">[54]</a></sup> The first flu pandemic of the 18th century started in 1729 in Russia in spring, spreading worldwide over the course of three years with distinct waves, the later ones being more lethal. The second flu pandemic of the 18th century was in 1781–1782, starting in China in autumn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-potter_45-5"><a href="#cite_note-potter-45">[45]</a></sup> From this pandemic, influenza became associated with sudden outbreaks of febrile illness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taubenberger_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-taubenberger-54">[54]</a></sup> The next flu pandemic was from 1830 to 1833, beginning in China in winter. This pandemic had a high attack rate, but the mortality rate was low.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hastings_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-hastings-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-potter_45-6"><a href="#cite_note-potter-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>A minor influenza pandemic occurred from 1847 to 1851 at the same time as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Third_cholera_pandemic" title="Third cholera pandemic">third cholera pandemic</a> and was the first flu pandemic to occur with vital statistics being recorded, so influenza mortality was clearly recorded for the first time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taubenberger_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-taubenberger-54">[54]</a></sup> Highly pathogenic avian influenza was recognized in 1878<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taubenberger_54-3"><a href="#cite_note-taubenberger-54">[54]</a></sup> and was soon linked to transmission to humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morens2_47-3"><a href="#cite_note-morens2-47">[47]</a></sup> By the time of the <a href="/wiki/1889%E2%80%931890_pandemic" title="1889–1890 pandemic">1889 pandemic</a>, which may have been caused by an H2N2 strain,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vijgen_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-vijgen-55">[55]</a></sup> the flu had become an easily recognizable disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morens2_47-4"><a href="#cite_note-morens2-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>Initially, the microbial agent responsible for influenza was incorrently identified in 1892 by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Friedrich_Johannes_Pfeiffer" title="Richard Friedrich Johannes Pfeiffer">R. F. J. Pfeiffer</a> as the bacteria species <i><a href="/wiki/Haemophilus_influenzae" title="Haemophilus influenzae">Haemophilus influenzae</a></i>, which retains "influenza" in its name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morens2_47-5"><a href="#cite_note-morens2-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taubenberger_54-4"><a href="#cite_note-taubenberger-54">[54]</a></sup> In the following years, the field of <a href="/wiki/Virology" title="Virology">virology</a> began to form as viruses were identified as the cause of many diseases. From 1901 to 1903, Italian and Austrian researchers were able to show that avian influenza, then called "fowl plague",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lycett_24-3"><a href="#cite_note-lycett-24">[24]</a></sup> was caused by a microscopic agent smaller than bacteria by using filters with pores too small for bacteria to pass through. The fundamental differences between viruses and bacteria, however, were not yet fully understood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taubenberger_54-5"><a href="#cite_note-taubenberger-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:W_curve.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="424" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/W_curve.png/220px-W_curve.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/W_curve.png/330px-W_curve.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/W_curve.png/440px-W_curve.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:W_curve.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The difference between the influenza mortality age distributions of the 1918 epidemic and normal epidemics. Deaths per 100,000 persons in each age group, United States, for the interpandemic years 1911–1917 (dashed line) and the pandemic year 1918 (solid line).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taubenberger2_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Taubenberger2-56">[56]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>From 1918 to 1920, the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_flu" title="Spanish flu">Spanish flu</a> pandemic became the most devastating influenza pandemic and one of the deadliest pandemics in history. The pandemic, probably caused by H1N1, likely began in the USA before spreading worldwide by soldiers during and after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_World_War" title="First World War">First World War</a>. The initial wave in the first half of 1918 was relatively minor and resembled past flu pandemics, but the second wave later that year had a much higher mortality rate,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-potter_45-7"><a href="#cite_note-potter-45">[45]</a></sup> accounting for most deaths. A third wave with lower mortality occurred in many places a few months after the second.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hastings_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-hastings-23">[23]</a></sup> By the end of 1920, it is estimated that about a third<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peteranderl_11-16"><a href="#cite_note-peteranderl-11">[11]</a></sup> to half of all people in the world had been infected, with tens of millions of deaths, disproportionately young adults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-potter_45-8"><a href="#cite_note-potter-45">[45]</a></sup> During the 1918 pandemic, the respiratory route of transmission was clearly identified<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hastings_23-3"><a href="#cite_note-hastings-23">[23]</a></sup> and influenza was shown to be caused by a "filter passer", not a bacterium, but there remained a lack of agreement about influenza's cause for another decade and research on influenza declined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taubenberger_54-6"><a href="#cite_note-taubenberger-54">[54]</a></sup> After the pandemic, H1N1 circulated in humans in seasonal form<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-76"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> up until the next pandemic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taubenberger_54-7"><a href="#cite_note-taubenberger-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1931, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Shope" title="Richard Shope">Richard Shope</a> published three papers identifying a virus as the cause of swine influenza, a then newly recognized disease among pigs that was first characterized during the second wave of the 1918 pandemic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-knobler_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-knobler-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taubenberger_54-8"><a href="#cite_note-taubenberger-54">[54]</a></sup> Shope's research reinvigorated research on human influenza, and many advances in virology, serology, immunology, experimental animal models, vaccinology, and immunotherapy have since arisen from influenza research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taubenberger_54-9"><a href="#cite_note-taubenberger-54">[54]</a></sup> Just two years after influenza viruses were discovered, in 1933, IAV was identified as the agent responsible for human influenza.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-knobler_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-knobler-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smith_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-smith-57">[57]</a></sup> Subtypes of IAV were discovered throughout the 1930s,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taubenberger_54-10"><a href="#cite_note-taubenberger-54">[54]</a></sup> and IBV was discovered in 1940.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asha_17-10"><a href="#cite_note-asha-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_World_War" title="Second World War">Second World War</a>, the US government worked on developing inactivated vaccines for influenza, resulting in the first influenza vaccine being licensed in 1945 in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-77"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> ICV was discovered two years later in 1947.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asha_17-11"><a href="#cite_note-asha-17">[17]</a></sup> In 1955, avian influenza was confirmed to be caused by IAV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lycett_24-4"><a href="#cite_note-lycett-24">[24]</a></sup> Four influenza pandemics have occurred since WWII, each less severe than the 1918 pandemic. The first of these was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asian_flu" title="Asian flu">Asian flu</a> from 1957 to 1958, caused by an H2N2 strain<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-78"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-saunders_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-saunders-37">[37]</a></sup> and beginning in China's <a href="/wiki/Yunnan" title="Yunnan">Yunnan</a> province. The number of deaths probably exceeded one million, mostly among the very young and very old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-potter_45-9"><a href="#cite_note-potter-45">[45]</a></sup> Notably, the 1957 pandemic was the first flu pandemic to occur in the presence of a global surveillance system and laboratories able to study the novel influenza virus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hastings_23-4"><a href="#cite_note-hastings-23">[23]</a></sup> After the pandemic, H2N2 was the IAV subtype responsible for seasonal influenza.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-79"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> The first antiviral drug against influenza, amantadine, was approved for use in 1966, with additional antiviral drugs being used since the 1990s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lampejo_4-14"><a href="#cite_note-lampejo-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1968, H3N2 was introduced into humans as a result of a reassortment between an avian H3N2 strain and an H2N2 strain that was circulating in humans. The novel H3N2 strain first emerged in Hong Kong and spread worldwide, causing the <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong_flu" title="Hong Kong flu">Hong Kong flu</a> pandemic, which resulted in 500,000–2,000,000 deaths. This was the first pandemic to spread significantly by air travel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allen_3-14"><a href="#cite_note-allen-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hastings_23-5"><a href="#cite_note-hastings-23">[23]</a></sup> H2N2 and H3N2 co-circulated after the pandemic until 1971 when H2N2 waned in prevalence and was completely replaced by H3N2.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allen_3-15"><a href="#cite_note-allen-3">[3]</a></sup> In 1977, H1N1 reemerged in humans, possibly after it was released from a freezer in a laboratory accident, and caused <a href="/wiki/1977_Russian_flu" title="1977 Russian flu">a pseudo-pandemic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hastings_23-6"><a href="#cite_note-hastings-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taubenberger_54-11"><a href="#cite_note-taubenberger-54">[54]</a></sup> Whether the 1977 "pandemic" deserves to be included in the natural history of flu pandemics is debatable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-potter_45-10"><a href="#cite_note-potter-45">[45]</a></sup> This H1N1 strain was antigenically similar to the H1N1 strains that circulated prior to 1957. Since 1977, both H1N1 and H3N2 have circulated in humans as part of seasonal influenza.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-80"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> In 1980, the current classification system used to subtype influenza viruses was introduced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heinen_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-heinen-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Airport_Thermographic_Camera.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Airport_Thermographic_Camera.jpg/220px-Airport_Thermographic_Camera.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Airport_Thermographic_Camera.jpg/330px-Airport_Thermographic_Camera.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Airport_Thermographic_Camera.jpg/440px-Airport_Thermographic_Camera.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Airport_Thermographic_Camera.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Thermal imaging camera and screen, photographed in an airport terminal in <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a> during the 2009 flu pandemic. Thermal imaging can detect elevated body temperature, one of the signs of swine flu.</div></div></div>
<p>At some point, IBV diverged into two lineages, named the B/Victoria-like and B/Yamagata-like lineages, both of which have been circulating in humans since 1983.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asha_17-12"><a href="#cite_note-asha-17">[17]</a></sup> In 1996, HPAI H5N1 was detected in <a href="/wiki/Guangdong" title="Guangdong">Guangdong</a>, China<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lycett_24-5"><a href="#cite_note-lycett-24">[24]</a></sup> and a year later emerged in poultry in Hong Kong, gradually spreading worldwide from there. A small H5N1 outbreak in humans in Hong Kong occurred then,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-steinhauer_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-steinhauer-30">[30]</a></sup> and sporadic human cases have occurred since 1997, carrying a high case fatality rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-li_15-9"><a href="#cite_note-li-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vemula_39-9"><a href="#cite_note-vemula-39">[39]</a></sup> The most recent flu pandemic was the <a href="/wiki/2009_swine_flu_pandemic" title="2009 swine flu pandemic">2009 swine flu pandemic</a>, which originated in Mexico and resulted in hundreds of thousands of deaths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hastings_23-7"><a href="#cite_note-hastings-23">[23]</a></sup> It was caused by a novel H1N1 strain that was a reassortment of human, swine, and avian influenza viruses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-joseph_16-3"><a href="#cite_note-joseph-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lampejo_4-15"><a href="#cite_note-lampejo-4">[4]</a></sup> The 2009 pandemic had the effect of replacing prior H1N1 strains in circulation with the novel strain but not any other influenza viruses. Consequently, H1N1, H3N2, and both IBV lineages have been in circulation in seasonal form since the 2009 pandemic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-81"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hastings_23-8"><a href="#cite_note-hastings-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lycett_24-6"><a href="#cite_note-lycett-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2011, IDV was discovered in pigs in Oklahoma, USA, and cattle were later identified as the primary reservoir of IDV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sederdahl_10-8"><a href="#cite_note-sederdahl-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asha_17-13"><a href="#cite_note-asha-17">[17]</a></sup> In the same year,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vemula_39-10"><a href="#cite_note-vemula-39">[39]</a></sup> avian H7N9 was detected in China and began to cause human infections in 2013, starting in <a href="/wiki/Shanghai" title="Shanghai">Shanghai</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anhui" title="Anhui">Anhui</a> and remaining mostly in China. HPAI H7N9 emerged sometime in 2016 and has occasionally infected humans incidentally. Other AIVs have less commonly infected humans since the 1990s, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H5N6" title="H5N6">H5N6</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H6N1" title="H6N1">H6N1</a>, H7N2-4, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H7N7" title="H7N7">H7N7</a>, and H10N7-8,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-li_15-10"><a href="#cite_note-li-15">[15]</a></sup> and HPAI H subtypes such as H5N1-3, H5N5-6, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H5N8" title="H5N8">H5N8</a> have begun to spread throughout much of the world since the 2010s. Future flu pandemics, which may be caused by an influenza virus of avian origin,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lycett_24-7"><a href="#cite_note-lycett-24">[24]</a></sup> are viewed as almost inevitable, and increased <a href="/wiki/Globalization" title="Globalization">globalization</a> has made it easier for novel viruses to spread,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hastings_23-9"><a href="#cite_note-hastings-23">[23]</a></sup> so there are continual efforts to prepare for future pandemics<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-knobler_53-3"><a href="#cite_note-knobler-53">[53]</a></sup> and improve the prevention and treatment of influenza.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-82"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h3>
<p>The word <i>influenza</i> comes from the <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a> word <i>influenza</i>, from medieval <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i>influentia</i>, originally meaning "visitation" or "influence". Terms such as <i>influenza di freddo</i>, meaning "influence of the cold", and <i>influenza di stelle</i>, meaning "influence of the stars" are attested from the 14th century. The latter referred to the disease's cause, which at the time was ascribed by some to unfavorable <a href="/wiki/Astrology" title="Astrology">astrological</a> conditions. As early as 1504, <i>influenza</i> began to mean a "visitation" or "outbreak" of any disease affecting many people in a single place at once. During an outbreak of influenza in 1743 that started in Italy and spread throughout Europe, the word reached the English language and was anglicized in pronunciation. Since the mid-1800s, <i>influenza</i> has also been used to refer to severe colds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-espinosa_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-espinosa-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-etymonline_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-etymonline-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oed_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-oed-61">[61]</a></sup> The shortened form of the word, "(the) flu", is first attested in 1839 as <i>flue</i> with the spelling <i>flu</i> first attested in 1893.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-etymflu_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-etymflu-62">[62]</a></sup> Other names that have been used for influenza include <i>epidemic catarrh</i>, <i>la grippe</i> from <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>, <i>sweating sickness</i>, and, especially when referring to the 1918 pandemic strain, <i>Spanish fever</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-calisher_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-calisher-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Influenza_virus_research.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2786" data-file-width="1966" decoding="async" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Influenza_virus_research.jpg/140px-Influenza_virus_research.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Influenza_virus_research.jpg/210px-Influenza_virus_research.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Influenza_virus_research.jpg/280px-Influenza_virus_research.jpg 2x" width="140"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Influenza_virus_research.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Professional examining a laboratory-grown reconstruction of the 1918 <a href="/wiki/Spanish_flu" title="Spanish flu">Spanish flu</a> virus in a <a href="/wiki/Biosafety_level" title="Biosafety level">biosafety level</a> 3 environment</div></div></div>
<p>Influenza research is wide-ranging and includes efforts to understand how influenza viruses enter hosts, the relationship between influenza viruses and bacteria, how influenza symptoms progress, and what make some influenza viruses deadlier than others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-niaid_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-niaid-64">[64]</a></sup> Non-structural proteins encoded by influenza viruses are periodically discovered and their functions are continually under research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hao_21-5"><a href="#cite_note-hao-21">[21]</a></sup> Past pandemics, and especially the 1918 pandemic, are the subject of much research to understand flu pandemics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-potter_45-11"><a href="#cite_note-potter-45">[45]</a></sup> As part of pandemic preparedness, the <a href="/wiki/Global_Influenza_Surveillance_and_Response_System" title="Global Influenza Surveillance and Response System">Global Influenza Surveillance and Response System</a> is a global network of laboratories that monitors influenza transmission and epidemiology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gisrs_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-gisrs-65">[65]</a></sup> Additional areas of research include ways to improve the diagnosis, treatment, and prevention of influenza.
</p><p>Existing diagnostic methods have a variety of limitations coupled with their advantages. For example, NATs have high sensitivity and specificity but are impractical in under-resourced regions due to their high cost, complexity, maintenance, and training required. Low-cost, portable RIDTs can rapidly diagnose influenza but have highly variable sensitivity and are unable to subtype IAV. As a result of these limitations and others, research into new diagnostic methods revolves around producing new methods that are cost-effective, less labor-intensive, and less complex than existing methods while also being able to differentiate influenza species and IAV subtypes. One approach in development are <a href="/wiki/Lab-on-a-chip" title="Lab-on-a-chip">lab-on-a-chips</a>, which are diagnostic devices that make use of a variety of diagnostic tests, such as RT-PCR and serological assays, in microchip form. These chips have many potential advantages, including high reaction efficiency, low energy consumption, and low waste generation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vemula_39-11"><a href="#cite_note-vemula-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>New antiviral drugs are also in development due to the elimination of adamantines as viable drugs and concerns over oseltamivir resistance. These include: NA inhibitors that can be injected intravenously, such as intravenous formulations of zanamivir; <a href="/wiki/Favipiravir" title="Favipiravir">favipiravir</a>, which is a polymerase inhibitor used against several RNA viruses; <a href="/wiki/Pimodivir" title="Pimodivir">pimodivir</a>, which prevents cap-binding required during viral transcription; and <a href="/wiki/Nitazoxanide" title="Nitazoxanide">nitazoxanide</a>, which inhibits HA maturation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-83"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peteranderl_11-17"><a href="#cite_note-peteranderl-11">[11]</a></sup> Reducing excess inflammation in the respiratory tract is also subject to much research since this is one of the primary mechanisms of influenza pathology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peteranderl_11-18"><a href="#cite_note-peteranderl-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kalil_13-11"><a href="#cite_note-kalil-13">[13]</a></sup> Other forms of therapy in development include monoclonal and polyclonal antibodies that target viral proteins, convalescent plasma, different approaches to modify the host antiviral response,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chow_32-15"><a href="#cite_note-chow-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nachbagauer_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-nachbagauer-66">[66]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Stem_cell" title="Stem cell">stem cell</a>-based therapies to repair lung damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peteranderl_11-19"><a href="#cite_note-peteranderl-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>Much research on LAIVs focuses on identifying genome sequences that can be deleted to create harmless influenza viruses in vaccines that still confer immunity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hao_21-6"><a href="#cite_note-hao-21">[21]</a></sup> The high variability and rapid evolution of influenza virus antigens, however, is a major obstacle in developing effective vaccines. Furthermore, it is hard to predict which strains will be in circulation during the next flu season, manufacturing a sufficient quantity of flu vaccines for the next season is difficult,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sautto_2-12"><a href="#cite_note-sautto-2">[2]</a></sup> LAIVs have limited efficacy, and repeated annual vaccination potentially has diminished efficacy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-84"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> For these reasons, "broadly-reactive" or "universal" flu vaccines are being researched that can provide protection against many or all influenza viruses. Approaches to develop such a vaccine include HA stalk-based methods such as chimeras that have the same stalk but different heads, HA head-based methods such as computationally optimized broadly neutralizing antigens, <a href="/wiki/Anti-idiotypic_vaccine" title="Anti-idiotypic vaccine">anti-idiotypic antibodies</a>, and vaccines to elicit immune responses to highly conserved viral proteins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sautto_2-13"><a href="#cite_note-sautto-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nachbagauer_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-nachbagauer-66">[66]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/MRNA_vaccine" title="MRNA vaccine">mRNA vaccines</a> to provide protection against influenza are also under research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_animals">In animals</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Birds">Birds</span></h3>
<p>Aquatic birds such as ducks, geese, shorebirds, and gulls are the primary reservoir of IAVs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-li_15-11"><a href="#cite_note-li-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-joseph_16-4"><a href="#cite_note-joseph-16">[16]</a></sup> In birds, AIVs may be either low pathogenic avian influenza (LPAI) viruses that produce little to no symptoms or highly pathogenic avian influenza (HPAI) viruses that cause severe illness. Symptoms of HPAI infection include lack of energy and appetite, decreased egg production, soft-shelled or misshapen eggs, swelling of the head, comb, wattles, and hocks, purple discoloration of wattles, combs, and legs, nasal discharge, coughing, sneezing, incoordination, and diarrhea. Birds infected with an HPAI virus may also die suddenly without any signs of infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-avian_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-avian-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>The distinction between LPAI and HPAI can generally be made based on how lethal an AIV is to chickens. At the genetic level, an AIV can be usually be identified as an HPAI virus if it has a multibasic cleavage site in the HA protein, which contains additional residues in the HA gene.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-joseph_16-5"><a href="#cite_note-joseph-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lycett_24-8"><a href="#cite_note-lycett-24">[24]</a></sup> Most AIVs are LPAI. Notable HPAI viruses include HPAI H5N1 and HPAI H7N9. HPAI viruses have been a major disease burden in the 21st century, resulting in the death of large numbers of birds. In H7N9's case, some circulating strains were originally LPAI but became HPAI by acquiring the HA multibasic cleavage site. Avian H9N2 is also of concern because although it is LPAI, it is a common donor of genes to H5N1 and H7N9 during reassortment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-85"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">Migratory birds</a> can spread influenza across long distances. An example of this was when an H5N1 strain in 2005 infected birds at <a href="/wiki/Qinghai_Lake" title="Qinghai Lake">Qinghai Lake</a>, China, which is a stopover and breeding site for many migratory birds, subsequently spreading the virus to more than 20 countries across Asia, Europe, and the Middle East.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-li_15-12"><a href="#cite_note-li-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lycett_24-9"><a href="#cite_note-lycett-24">[24]</a></sup> AIVs can be transmitted from wild birds to domestic free-range ducks and in turn to poultry through contaminated water, aerosols, and fomites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-86"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> Ducks therefore act as key intermediates between wild and domestic birds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lycett_24-10"><a href="#cite_note-lycett-24">[24]</a></sup> Transmission to poultry typically occurs in backyard farming and live animal markets where multiple species interact with each other. From there, AIVs can spread to poultry farms in the absence of adequate biosecurity. Among poultry, HPAI transmission occurs through aerosols and contaminated feces,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-87"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> cages, feed, and dead animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-li_15-13"><a href="#cite_note-li-15">[15]</a></sup> Back-transmission of HPAI viruses from poultry to wild birds has occurred and is implicated in mass die-offs and intercontinental spread.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-joseph_16-6"><a href="#cite_note-joseph-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>AIVs have occasionally infected humans through aerosols, fomites, and contaminated water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-88"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> Direction transmission from wild birds is rare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lycett_24-11"><a href="#cite_note-lycett-24">[24]</a></sup> Instead, most transmission involves domestic poultry, mainly chickens, ducks, and geese but also a variety of other birds such as guinea fowl, partridge, pheasants, and quails.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-joseph_16-7"><a href="#cite_note-joseph-16">[16]</a></sup> The primary risk factor for infection with AIVs is exposure to birds in farms and live poultry markets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-li_15-14"><a href="#cite_note-li-15">[15]</a></sup> Typically, infection with an AIV has an incubation period of 3–5 days but can be up to 9 days. H5N1 and H7N9 cause severe lower respiratory tract illness, whereas other AIVs such as H9N2 cause a more mild upper respiratory tract illness, commonly with conjunctivitis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-89"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> Limited transmission of avian H2, H5-7, H9, and H10 subtypes from one person to another through respiratory droplets, aerosols, and fomites has occurred,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-90"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sautto_2-14"><a href="#cite_note-sautto-2">[2]</a></sup> but sustained human-to-human transmission of AIVs has not occurred. Before 2013, H5N1 was the most common AIV to infect humans. Since then, H7N9 has been responsible for most human cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-li_15-15"><a href="#cite_note-li-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pigs">Pigs</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ChineseFluInspectors.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="443" data-file-width="441" decoding="async" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/ChineseFluInspectors.JPG/170px-ChineseFluInspectors.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/ChineseFluInspectors.JPG/255px-ChineseFluInspectors.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/ChineseFluInspectors.JPG/340px-ChineseFluInspectors.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ChineseFluInspectors.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chinese inspectors checking airline passengers for fevers, a common symptom of swine flu</div></div></div>
<p>Influenza in pigs is a respiratory disease similar to influenza in humans and is found worldwide. Asymptomatic infections are common. Symptoms typically appear 1–3 days after infection and include fever, lethargy, anorexia, weight loss, labored breathing, coughing, sneezing, and nasal discharge. In sows, pregnancy may be aborted. Complications include secondary infections and potentially fatal <a href="/wiki/Bronchopneumonia" title="Bronchopneumonia">bronchopneumonia</a>. Pigs become contagious within a day of infection and typically spread the virus for 7–10 days, which can spread rapidly within a herd. Pigs usually recover from infection within 3–7 days after symptoms appear. Prevention and control measures include inactivated vaccines and culling infected herds. The influenza viruses usually responsible for swine flu are IAV subtypes H1N1, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H1N2" title="H1N2">H1N2</a>, and H3N2.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-swine_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-swine-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some IAVs can be transmitted via aerosols from pigs to humans and vice versa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-91"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> Furthermore, pigs, along with bats and quails,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asha_17-14"><a href="#cite_note-asha-17">[17]</a></sup> are recognized as a mixing vessel of influenza viruses because they have both α-2,3 and α-2,6 sialic acid receptors in their respiratory tract. Because of that, both avian and mammalian influenza viruses can infect pigs. If co-infection occurs, then reassortment is possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-joseph_16-8"><a href="#cite_note-joseph-16">[16]</a></sup> A notable example of this was the reassortment of a swine, avian, and human influenza virus in 2009, resulting in a novel H1N1 strain that caused the 2009 flu pandemic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-joseph_16-9"><a href="#cite_note-joseph-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lampejo_4-16"><a href="#cite_note-lampejo-4">[4]</a></sup> Spillover events from humans to pigs, however, appear to be more common than from pigs to humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-joseph_16-10"><a href="#cite_note-joseph-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_animals">Other animals</span></h3>
<p>Influenza viruses have been found in many other animals, including cattle, horses, dogs, cats, and marine mammals. Nearly all IAVs are apparently descended from ancestral viruses in birds. The exception are bat influenza-like viruses, which have an uncertain origin. These bat viruses have HA and NA subtypes H17, H18, N10, and N11. <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=H17N10&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="H17N10 (page does not exist)">H17N10</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=H18N11&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="H18N11 (page does not exist)">H18N11</a> are unable to reassort with other IAVs, but they are still able to replicate in other mammals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-92"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> AIVs sometimes crossover into mammals. For example, in late 2016 to early 2017, an avian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H7N2" title="H7N2">H7N2</a> strain was found to be infecting cats in New York.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-93"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>Equine IAVs include H7N7 and two lineages<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-94"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H3N8" title="H3N8">H3N8</a>. H7N7, however, has not been detected in horses since the late 1970s,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mccauley_19-7"><a href="#cite_note-mccauley-19">[19]</a></sup> so it may have become extinct in horses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-joseph_16-11"><a href="#cite_note-joseph-16">[16]</a></sup> H3N8 in equines spreads via aerosols and causes respiratory illness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krammer_1-95"><a href="#cite_note-krammer-1">[1]</a></sup> Equine H3N8 perferentially binds to α-2,3 sialic acids, so horses are usually considered dead-end hosts, but transmission to dogs and camels has occurred, raising concerns that horses may be mixing vessels for reassortment. In canines, the only IAVs in circulation are equine-derived H3N8 and avian-derived H3N2. Canine H3N8 has not been observed to reassort with other subtypes. H3N2 has a much broader host range and can reassort with H1N1 and H5N1. An isolated case of H6N1 likely from a chicken was found infecting a dog, so other AIVs may emerge in canines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-joseph_16-12"><a href="#cite_note-joseph-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other mammals to be infected by IAVs include H7N7 and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=H4N5&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="H4N5 (page does not exist)">H4N5</a> in seals, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=H1N3&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="H1N3 (page does not exist)">H1N3</a> in whales, and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=H10N4&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="H10N4 (page does not exist)">H10N4</a> and H3N2 in minks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mccauley_19-8"><a href="#cite_note-mccauley-19">[19]</a></sup> Various mutations have been identified that are associated with AIVs adapting to mammals. Since HA proteins vary in which sialic acids they bind to, mutations in the HA receptor binding site can allow AIVs to infect mammals. Other mutations include mutations affecting which sialic acids NA proteins cleave and a mutation in the PB2 polymerase subunit that improves tolerance of lower temperatures in mammalian respiratory tracts and enhances RNP assembly by stabilizing NP and PB2 binding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-joseph_16-13"><a href="#cite_note-joseph-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>IBV is mainly found in humans but has also been detected in pigs, dogs, horses, and seals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asha_17-15"><a href="#cite_note-asha-17">[17]</a></sup> Likewise, ICV primarily infects humans but has been observed in pigs, dogs, cattle, and dromedary camels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sederdahl_10-9"><a href="#cite_note-sederdahl-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asha_17-16"><a href="#cite_note-asha-17">[17]</a></sup> IDV causes an influenza-like illness in pigs but its impact in its natural reservoir, cattle, is relatively unknown. It may cause respiratory disease resembling human influenza on its own, or it may be part of a <a href="/wiki/Bovine_respiratory_disease" title="Bovine respiratory disease">bovine respiratory disease</a> (BRD) complex with other pathogens during co-infection. BRD is a concern for the cattle industry, so IDV's possible involvement in BRD has led to research on vaccines for cattle that can provide protection against IDV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asha_17-17"><a href="#cite_note-asha-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-su_18-10"><a href="#cite_note-su-18">[18]</a></sup> Two antigenic lineages are in circulation: D/swine/Oklahoma/1334/2011 (D/OK) and D/bovine/Oklahoma/660/2013 (D/660).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asha_17-18"><a href="#cite_note-asha-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
